  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_dffa0df4-aa64-4228-8990-01116d8e4edf'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

She kisses like a movie star and knows everything about every film ever made. She's also dead and waiting in the Rosebud Theater for Alec Sheldon one afternoon in 1945.... <br /><br />Arthur Roth is a lonely kid with big ideas and a gift for attracting abuse. It isn't easy to make friends when you're the only inflatable boy in town.... <br /><br />Francis is unhappy. Francis was human once, but that was then. Now he's an eight-foot-tall locust and everyone in Calliphora will tremble when they hear him sing....<br /><br />John Finney is locked in a basement that's stained with the blood of half a dozen other murdered children. In the cellar with him is an antique telephone, long since disconnected, but which rings at night with calls from the dead....<br /><br />The past isn't dead. It isn't even past...</span>

<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1312721-i-m-still-trying-to-digest-my-father-s">I&#39;m still trying to digest &quot;My Father&#39;s Mask.&quot; It wasn&#39;t necessarily one of my favorites in this collection, but it was one of the most memorable. I&#39;m not exactly sure what to make of it beyond it being a creepy slice of surrealism. I&#39;d love to hear what anyone else thought about it. Any ideas?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1312721'><a id="like_count_community_question_1312721" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1312721?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">2 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F373915.20th_Century_Ghosts">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1312721-i-m-still-trying-to-digest-my-father-s">2 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1312721-i-m-still-trying-to-digest-my-father-s">See all 3 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/22520502-michele"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/22520502-michele">Michele</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Warning!!! Spoilers ahead!!<br /><br />I just read "My Father's Mask" yesterday, and not to be too literary or anything, it creeped me out. I don't know what to m<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Warning!!! Spoilers ahead!!<br /><br />I just read "My Father's Mask" yesterday, and not to be too literary or anything, it creeped me out. I don't know what to make of it either, but the mask symbolism reminds us we never really know one another. And Jack certainly did not know his parents and in the end anything he knew of his father he forgot. <br /><br />The card game was interesting because after saying he could recognize his mother, even with a mask on, he failed to recognize her or his father. Another reference to never really knowing another person. The card game also continues the theme of games, which his mother plays continually both to mask reality and to give Jack a road map to navigate their peculiarly surreal (your very apt word) reality. <br /><br />His father sacrifices himself for Jack (and perhaps his mother) not only physically by giving himself to the card woman but also metaphorically (not quite sure if that is the word I want), as even memories of him fade from Jack's consciousness to be replaced by something else. (Probably a reference to the very human habit of our projecting what we think someone is like over who they actually are.)<br /><br />Now if you want to get all psychoanalytical about the story you can replace Jack with Joe and his parents become Tabitha and Stephen King. The surrealness becomes what growing up with a famous horror-writing father is like. The games reference the fact that both his father and his mother are novelists and exist in the world of fiction. Joe is perhaps slyly implying his mother is the better writer since she seems so much better at playing the games than his father. The card woman would become Stephen's drug addiction that he succumbs to. His attempt to warn Joe that he will be leaving and not to forget him may be his consciousness that the drugs are winning. His mother's manipulating almost the entire situation beginning with the drive to the cabin is Tabitha's shielding him (and his siblings) from his father's addictions. In this story, unlike in real life, his father is lost forever. The overwriting of the memories as they leave the cabin may be how Joe sees the family memories of that time when his father had problems. They gloss over the bad and focus on (and perhaps invent) only the good. (It also may be simply the perspective, his mother's view of that time will differ significantly from his for a number of reasons.)<br /><br />Two more thoughts (if you are still with me and not rolling your eyes). Jack is a reference to the jack in a deck of cards. Jack represents someone of the lower classes (in France) or a servant of royalty (in England. Thank you Wikipedia!) In this story we can perhaps assume that the mother is the queen, the father is the king, and the narrator is their son, the jack.<br /><br />And, finally, I keep thinking about the boy on the velocipede. I can not figure that one out. Is he there to add weirdness, does his appearing on the path mean something (if Jack is playing cards with his parents, is the boy on the bike Jack?), or is he just there as part of the landscape? Also, the card woman. What exactly does she symbolize? <br /><br />Sorry if this was more than you bargained for when you asked for ideas, but, like you, I found it very unsettling and memorable and once I started writing about it I realized how great the story was. There is a lot in it and that is a testament to Joe Hill's writing.<br /><br />Keep on reading!<br /><a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_460232718" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/460232718" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/460232718?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=weird-fiction">weird-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=ghost-stories">ghost-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer460232718" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1845711215735115209"><br/>Seldom does a collection of weird stories feature a style so accomplished, a range of tone and mood so broad, or a generosity so profound. Hill, the son of Stephen King, inherits his father's empathy for the ordeals of childhood as well as his artfulness in constructing a tale, but he also possesses a warmth and an elegance all his own. <br/><br/>At times his stories are chilling and gripping like the horror fiction of King ("The Black Phone"), but at other times they are gentle and elegiac like Bradbury </span>
  <span id="freeText1845711215735115209" style="display:none"><br />Seldom does a collection of weird stories feature a style so accomplished, a range of tone and mood so broad, or a generosity so profound. Hill, the son of Stephen King, inherits his father's empathy for the ordeals of childhood as well as his artfulness in constructing a tale, but he also possesses a warmth and an elegance all his own. <br /><br />At times his stories are chilling and gripping like the horror fiction of King ("The Black Phone"), but at other times they are gentle and elegiac like Bradbury ("Better Than Home") or quirky and humorous like Vonnegut ("Bobby Conroy Comes Back from the Dead"). And then there are times when Hill's writing is so good that comparisons don't arise. There is the self-aware, post-modernist work in which a well-known anthologist gets savagely schooled by a contributor about what constitutes "The Best in Modern Horror," the unsettling tale about the effect of family disguises and games on a son ("My Father's Mask"), the powerful, sustained novella about an autistic brother who can construct marvelous, imprisoning labyrinths, a story which never loses its horror or its humanity ("Voluntary Committal"), or the masterpiece--I do not use the term lightly--about an "inflatable" childhood friend ("Pop Art") that has something to teach every reader about disability and transcendence.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1845711215735115209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_39354251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39354251" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39354251?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39354251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15659489411451935747">The crab apple does not fall far from the poisoned tree. While the sensibility is his own, it is eminently clear that Joe Hill has been gifted with DNA predisposed to horror greatness. <br/><br/>Before Heart-Shaped Box, Hill wrote short stories. Maybe he still does.<br/>I was immediately taken with his ability to draw the reader in. For most of the stories here, I quickly felt that I could settle back in my chair and let Hill lead me wherever he wanted. He engages quickly and strongly. He is having a lot of fu</span>
  <span id="freeText15659489411451935747" style="display:none">The crab apple does not fall far from the poisoned tree. While the sensibility is his own, it is eminently clear that Joe Hill has been gifted with DNA predisposed to horror greatness. <br /><br />Before Heart-Shaped Box, Hill wrote short stories. Maybe he still does.<br />I was immediately taken with his ability to draw the reader in. For most of the stories here, I quickly felt that I could settle back in my chair and let Hill lead me wherever he wanted. He engages quickly and strongly. He is having a lot of fun here with standard horror fare. Best New Horror looks at both the world of small magazine/journal publishing and Hollywood movie clichés. Movies come to the fore again in 20th Century Ghost, in which a specter appears in an old movie house to select patrons. There’s no place like home. I was less impressed with Pop Art, although I admire its daring. The narrator is a boy with an inflatable friend and even in a book of ghost stories it was too allegorical to allow any emotional engagement. You Will Hear the Locust Sing takes a Kafkaesque look at social ostracism and adolescent rage with a dose of apocalyptic religiosity. Abraham’s Boys brings Van Helsing to America, wonders how he might be welcomed in the New World, and what sort of father he might be. Better Than Home looks at a Billy Martin sort of volcanic baseball manager. The Cape offers a nice twist on wish fulfillment. Voluntary Commitment is the story here that most makes one recall Hill’s father. Adolescent boys, danger, fear, and magic. Hill stretches a bit in defining what constitutes a ghost story. Some, frankly, don’t qualify. But that is a quibble. This is a collection of mostly interesting tales, well told. I am eager to read more of Hill’s work. He is a very bright light on the horror scene.<br /><br />=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br /><br />Links to the author’s <a href="https://www.joehillfiction.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">personal</a>, <a href="https://www.instagram.com/joe_hill/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> and <a href="http://joehillsthrills.tumblr.com/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumblr</a> pages<br /><br /><b>Other Joe Hill books I have reviewed:</b><br />-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2923249839" rel="nofollow noopener">Full Throttle</a><br />-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2058840059" rel="nofollow noopener">Strange Weather</a><br />-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1567110060" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fireman</a><br />-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/483336047" target="_blank" by his dad</b><br />-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3000574256" rel="nofollow noopener">The Institute</a><br />-----<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1110724484" <span id="freeTextContainer7544620916664897472">I really enjoyed this compilation of short stories by <i>Joe Hill</i>! They are not all horror so know that going in when you read this.<br/><br/>Overall, I would give this compliation <b>4 stars!</b><br/><br/>I've broken down all the short stories below and what I liked about them. Some were "okay" and not a favorite but the writing was still good.<br/><i>Joe Hill</i> has a fantastic imagination and his writing is top notch in my books!<br/><br/><b>Best New Horror</b> – 5 stars!<br/>This was one of my favorite short stories.   <span id="freeText7544620916664897472" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this compilation of short stories by <i>Joe Hill</i>! They are not all horror so know that going in when you read this.<br /><br />Overall, I would give this compliation <b>4 stars!</b><br /><br />I've broken down all the short stories below and what I liked about them. Some were "okay" and not a favorite but the writing was still good.<br /><i>Joe Hill</i> has a fantastic imagination and his writing is top notch in my books!<br /><br /><b>Best New Horror</b> – 5 stars!<br />This was one of my favorite short stories. I loved the take on the producer trying to find the new horror writer and how it all goes downhill at the end.<br /><br /><b>20th Century Ghost</b> – 4 stars<br />Eerie and a bit romantic. Loved the setting of an old movie theater!<br /><br /><b>Pop Art</b> – 2.5 stars<br />Still a bit confused by the balloon people, but the ending was a nice touch.<br /><br /><b>You Will Hear the Locust Sing</b> - 3.5 stars<br />It was gross and felt like a creature feature. I enjoyed this one for what it was!<br /><br /><b>Abraham’s Boys</b> - 5 stars!<br />I loved this one and it was a favorite! It was a nice, new take on Abraham Van Helsing and vampires.<br /><br /><b>Better Than Home</b> - 2 stars<br />Ehh…I didn’t see the point in this one for the compilation. Maybe looking through the eyes of someone who sees the world differently? I thought the kid might be autistic.<br /><br /><b>The Black Phone</b> - 3.5 stars<br />It had a John Wayne Gacy feel to the book and I was happy with the end!<br /><br /><b>In the Rundown</b> - 2 stars<br />The writing was good but wasn’t great. It just felt unfinished. Ehh…<br /><br /><b>The Cape</b> - 3 stars<br />I enjoyed the magical realism to this story.<br /><br /><b>Last Breath</b> - 4 stars<br />Creepy and great idea to bottle the last breath of someone when they die! I liked the idea of this one.<br /><br /><b>Dead-Wood</b> - 2.5 stars<br />I would give this a higher rating if it was longer. I just wanted more from this one! What a cool start and concept though.<br /><br /><b>The Widow’s Breakfast</b> - 3.5 stars<br />I liked how this one ended. It was a bit open and creepy. You’re not quite sure what will happen to Killian with the girls.<br /><br /><b>Bobby Conroy Comes Back from the Dead</b> - 3 stars<br />Not a bad short story about being an extra in a movie and finding lost connections again.<br /><br /><b>My Father’s Mask</b> - 3.5 stars<br />I felt like I was on acid reading this. I’m not sure what the playing cards theme was about or all the masks. Very weird but still creepy and I liked it!<br /><br /><b>Voluntary Committal</b> - 4 stars<br />I really liked this one about an autistic brother and the magical worlds he built from his mind. He would build buildings or forts from boxes, dominos or Dixie cups.<br />It was a very magical story about the loss of innocence and seeing the world through the eyes of someone who looks at it in a different way.</span>

<div id="review_1999736898" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1999736898" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1999736898?book_show_action=true">May 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1999736898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10282766678080585794">When I read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15729539.NOS4A2' title='NOS4A2 by Joe Hill' rel='nofollow noopener'>NOS4A2</a>, I had pegged <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/88506.Joe_Hill' title='Joe Hill' rel='nofollow noopener'>Joe Hill</a> as a horror writer of the calibre of his celebrated father. When I started this book, I expected much of the same fare: however, I was pleasantly surprised. “20th Century Ghosts” is less about ghosts than other sorts of boogeymen: more subtle, more scary, and all definitely weird.<br/><br/>It starts off with a traditional story (“Best New Horror”) about a horror anthology editor trapped in something like one of his own tales: despite the story-within-the-story str</span>
  <span id="freeText10282766678080585794" style="display:none">When I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15729539.NOS4A2" title="NOS4A2 by Joe Hill" rel="nofollow noopener">NOS4A2</a>, I had pegged <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/88506.Joe_Hill" title="Joe Hill" rel="nofollow noopener">Joe Hill</a> as a horror writer of the calibre of his celebrated father. When I started this book, I expected much of the same fare: however, I was pleasantly surprised. “20th Century Ghosts” is less about ghosts than other sorts of boogeymen: more subtle, more scary, and all definitely weird.<br /><br />It starts off with a traditional story (“Best New Horror”) about a horror anthology editor trapped in something like one of his own tales: despite the story-within-the-story structure of the first part, this tale becomes highly predictable towards the end and falls rather flat. In fact, all the stories using familiar tropes in this collection (“The Black Phone” about a serial killer, and the novella “Voluntary Committal” about an idiot savant and tunnels to distant dimensions) suffer from predictability. Joe Hill comes into his own, I think, when he throws away the manual and drifts off into the territory of the weird.<br /><br />Take “Pop Art”, a story of an inflatable boy – no, not a toy but a living being born to flesh and blood parents, in an alternate universe where such things are possible: or “My Father’s Mask”, where the goofy family game played by a family slowly slides into reality: or “The Cape”, where the superhero myth takes a violent twist. None of these can be called horror, but we are definitely in the land of Kafka. Speaking of whom, there is a sort of tribute to one of his all-time great stories, <i>The Metamorphosis</i> – in “You Will Hear the Locust Sing”, a youngster in the same dilemma as Greg Samsa decides to take his life in hands, with true American fortitude.<br /><br />The question “what happened after Count Dracula was defeated?” is answered in “Abraham’s Boys”, but it may not be everyone’s cup of tea to see this negative take on a classic of the horror genre. I found it powerful, however – especially how the author depicts the tortured world of the Van Helsing children with such cruel clarity. Indeed, the disturbed and often terrifying world of misfit children and broken homes is a common theme in this collection – it permeates eight of the sixteen stories. Is it so bad in America now, one wonders.<br /><br />There are a stories without any element of horror (“Better Than Home”, “The Widow’s Breakfast”, “Bobby Conroy Comes Back from the Dead”) which are still unsettling because of the human misery left unsaid underneath the bland narration. This is one thing that Joe Hill does beautifully; his prose is sometimes so prosaic and unemotional, almost Hemingway-esque, still it conveys a depth of feeling.<br /><br />Among all these stories, the title story stands out as the only one where a traditional ghost appears. But there is nothing frightening about this ghost – in fact, she is rather endearing and sexy. Through her, the author manages to convey what the magic of movies was before television forced itself into our living rooms.<br /><br />If you are a connoisseur of the unconventional, a fan of the disturbing and likes your literature to keep you awake than put you to sleep, this is the book for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10282766678080585794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1217223075" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1217223075" />
    <a title="Kelli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4146551-kelli"><img alt="Kelli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413330332p2/4146551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1217223075?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelli" href="/user/show/4146551-kelli">Kelli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146551-kelli?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4146551-kelli?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1217223075" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8277449410640771777">Joe Hill makes me feel like I'm seven again. More specifically, I'm seven and he is the nine-year-old kid down the street hanging out with me in my tree fort telling me scary stories and urban legends that he <i>swears</i> are true. His voice is hushed, as if he is letting me in on a little known secret and I'm not quite sure whether to believe him but I'm mesmerized and a little afraid.<br/><br/>Joe Hill gets the underdog and the misunderstood. He also writes childhood well, but it is when writing that mislabel</span>
  <span id="freeText8277449410640771777" style="display:none">Joe Hill makes me feel like I'm seven again. More specifically, I'm seven and he is the nine-year-old kid down the street hanging out with me in my tree fort telling me scary stories and urban legends that he <i>swears</i> are true. His voice is hushed, as if he is letting me in on a little known secret and I'm not quite sure whether to believe him but I'm mesmerized and a little afraid.<br /><br />Joe Hill gets the underdog and the misunderstood. He also writes childhood well, but it is when writing that mislabeled person that he truly shines. One story called Pop Art was not scary in the traditional sense. It was its own kind of brilliant, somehow age-old and refreshingly new. <br /><br />This short story collection is quite good. I listened to the audio and the narrator is perfect, which adds an important element in this genre. As with any collection of shorts, I liked some more than others but I give this extra credit for variety and interesting endings. 3.75 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8277449410640771777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1640542690" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1640542690" />
    <a title="Ron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43881851-ron"><img alt="Ron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557265090p2/43881851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1640542690?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ron" href="/user/show/43881851-ron">Ron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=short-story">short-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43881851-ron?shelf=anthology-collection">anthology-collection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1640542690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8388378843198446804">More often than not, Joe Hill characterizes the child’s life within these stories. I think that’s where our imaginations and fears are strongest, and when things scare us the most. When we are grown, most of that is gone…until we pick up a certain book. Shit, I love the way Hill writes. It is so utterly readable. He makes me believe in ghosts and the supernatural, but even more in the power of a simple story. I say that because not even half of the stories included here are meant to be frighteni</span>
  <span id="freeText8388378843198446804" style="display:none">More often than not, Joe Hill characterizes the child’s life within these stories. I think that’s where our imaginations and fears are strongest, and when things scare us the most. When we are grown, most of that is gone…until we pick up a certain book. Shit, I love the way Hill writes. It is so utterly readable. He makes me believe in ghosts and the supernatural, but even more in the power of a simple story. I say that because not even half of the stories included here are meant to be frightening. Some are just about people, like you and me. So, I have no qualms in giving 5 stars to 20th Century Ghosts. There were a few lesser-than-great short stories in here, but others were like shining lights, out of the park homeruns. Following is a short take on each:<br /><br /><b>Best New Horror</b> - Includes a story within the story called the Buttonboy, written by a truly creepy amateur writer named Kilrue, whose inspiration is very real. In this one, the getting there may be better than the finish.<br /><b>20th Century Ghost</b> - Very much a character-driven piece about a young woman who haunts the Rosebud theatre, and Alec Sheldon the man who spends his life there because of it. Not meant to be frightening, although I kept waiting for the other shoe to drop. A satisfying climax.<br /><b>Pop Art</b> - Such a wildly different kind of story. I didn’t know at first if it was all in the kid’s head, or some sort of magical realism. I grew to care and wanted to defend this boy made of plastic and air. Such good stuff with a lesson for life. I wanted to effing cry. Is this the best of the bunch? <b>A++++</b><br /><b>You Will Hear the Locust Sing</b> - An Ode to Franz Kafka and The Metamorphosis, but far darker and insidious. Didn’t think I would like it, but I surely did.<br /><b>Abraham’s Boys</b> - Is their father a vampire hunter, or simply an abusive man who found an excuse to kill? A different take on a vampire tale. One that I enjoyed, but the story could have been stretched with a few more pages. I want to know more.<br /><b>Better Than Home</b> - Some of this is summed up in the title. It’s a lovely, short analogy for one dad’s relationship with his boy. I had to think a bit about this one at the end. Baseball, fathers. Then it hit me. Gosh this was exceptional and beautifully understated.<br /><b>The Black Phone</b> - A boy is abducted by the fat man with the black balloons. Just the right amount of tension and paranormal in here. I am always more scared for the young and innocent characters. Also, looking back on childhood, I remember the belief in the supernatural that vanishes with age.<br /><b>In the Rundown</b> - The tables are turned in a very different sort of way for a young guy who doesn’t realize he’s judgmental a bully. I wasn’t sure about the meaning, or the point. Maybe there isn’t one for some situations.<br /><b>The Cape</b> - Did the lucky blanket that became his cape make this boy fly? Now an adult, does any of that child’s wonder remain in Eric’s life? What I thought would be an introspective piece became the unexpected. Has a wow ending that had my mouth hanging open!<br /><b>Last Breath</b> - Enter a museum that collects the last breaths sounds (or silences) people make. High creep factor. Short and not sweet.<br /><b>Dead-Wood</b> - The runt of the litter.<br /><b>A Widow’s Breakfast</b> - A straight tale with a hint of darkness, but really more literature than horror. I’ll call it a parable. A mourning vagrant (really sounds like too harsh a word) finds the gift <br /><b>Bobby Conroy Comes Back from the Dead</b> - Bobby bumps into his high school flame as zombie-extras on a film shoot at the local mall. Harriet is now married and Bobby was the “most-likely-to-succeed” bound for Hollywood. Things don’t always work out as we hope. Hill writes about life in so many different ways. Simple yet deep. <i>“Everyone wants a do-over”</i><br /><b>My Father’s Mask</b> - Super weird tale told from the viewpoint of a 13-year old with parents I could not fathom. Not without its moments of realness. <br /><b>Voluntary Commital</b> - The longest of the book at 50 pages, and in some ways it is the most disturbing. Nolan tells the story of his younger, autistic brother Morris, his friend Eddie, and how they both went missing. It makes believe that holes can be made that lead to somewhere else. A place from where there is no return. Good from start to finish, and one of the best in this collection. If I had to judge, I’d give top honors to Pop Art and Better than Home, but this is almost too close to call.<br /><br />PS. Do not skip the acknowledgements.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8388378843198446804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2321721199" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2321721199" />
    <a title="Paul O&#39;Neill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17331573-paul-o-neill"><img alt="Paul O&#39;Neill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462480730p2/17331573.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2321721199?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul O&#39;Neill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul O&#39;Neill" href="/user/show/17331573-paul-o-neill">Paul O&#39;Neill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2321721199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7200936249843865836">A fantastic collection of stories, tightly written (like a certain family member). Short stories aren’t usually my thing but between this and his more recent collection of four novellas, Strange Weather, Joe Hill has maybe changed my mind. <br/><br/>Some stories were better than others but there wasn’t a single dudd in this collection. My favourites were Pop Art (boy makes friends with Art, a balloon), Better Than Home (a nice Father-Son story) and the longer story at the end of the book, Voluntary Commit</span>
  <span id="freeText7200936249843865836" style="display:none">A fantastic collection of stories, tightly written (like a certain family member). Short stories aren’t usually my thing but between this and his more recent collection of four novellas, Strange Weather, Joe Hill has maybe changed my mind. <br /><br />Some stories were better than others but there wasn’t a single dudd in this collection. My favourites were Pop Art (boy makes friends with Art, a balloon), Better Than Home (a nice Father-Son story) and the longer story at the end of the book, Voluntary Commital (em... can’t really describe without spoiling). I’d go as far to say that Pop Art is one of the best things I’ve read all year and is up there with Loaded which is one of Hill’s stories in Strange Weather. <br /><br />Most of these stories are horror stories, although they are not blunt but rather subtle and scary as hell. I think I read the scariest thing ever in Best New Horror:<br /><i>‘He made holes in my eyes and he said after he did it he saw my soul rush out. He said it made a sound like when you blow on an empty Coke bottle, real pretty. Then he put these over my eyes to keep my life trapped inside.’ As he speaks, Jim touches the smiley-face buttons. ‘He wants to see how long I can live without a soul inside me.’</i><br /><br />Although mostly horror, Hill has some nicer stories in this collection and all of the stories are about the people in them and not just an excuse for some horror porn like other collections. <br /><br /><i>You get an astronaut’s life whether you want it or not. Leave it all behind for a world you know nothing about. That’s just the deal.</i><br /><br />A worthwhile read from one of my favourite authors.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7200936249843865836" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Sandi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811687-sandi"><img alt="Sandi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404709147p2/811687.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32128790?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandi" href="/user/show/811687-sandi">Sandi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061147982" href="/book/show/4443319-20th-century-ghosts">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=2008">2008</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32128790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4324509436186552706">Short story collections can be hit or miss. Some stories may be wonderful, others may be clunkers. Every story in “20th Century Ghosts” is wonderful, some are even superlative. While Hill is a horror writer, not all of the stories in this book are horror. And, some of the ones that deal with the typical subjects of horror are not horrific. To me, the best stories in this collection are:<br/><br/>“20th Century Ghost”, a touching story about a haunted movie theater.<br/><br/>“Abraham’s Boys”, a tale about teen-age re</span>
  <span id="freeText4324509436186552706" style="display:none">Short story collections can be hit or miss. Some stories may be wonderful, others may be clunkers. Every story in “20th Century Ghosts” is wonderful, some are even superlative. While Hill is a horror writer, not all of the stories in this book are horror. And, some of the ones that deal with the typical subjects of horror are not horrific. To me, the best stories in this collection are:<br /><br />“20th Century Ghost”, a touching story about a haunted movie theater.<br /><br />“Abraham’s Boys”, a tale about teen-age rebellion in unusual circumstances.<br /><br />“Better than Home”, a sweet story told from the perspective of the autistic son of a baseball coach. It really tugged at my heart.<br /><br />“My Father’s Mask”, a very creepy warped, fairytale-like story that left me with the shivers. <br /><br />“Voluntary Committal”, an indescribable fantasy/horror story about brotherly love. <br /><br />Be sure to read the acknowledgments at the end. Hill throws in a terrific little story as an encore. <br /><br />I have to admit that I am now a Joe Hill fan and will be on the lookout for his next book. His work isn’t formulaic; and, as some of his short stories prove, he leans more towards psychological horror than blood &amp; gore. His work is rich and complex. His characters are multidimensional and realistic. I highly recommend Joe Hill’s work for anyone who wants a good read.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4324509436186552706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

When the stories hit, they hit hard and wonderfully and had me chortling with glee. Best Horror Story was easily my favorite.   <span id="freeText3355621277062826949" style="display:none">Oddly enough, this collection was something of a hit or miss with me. When the stories hit, they hit hard and wonderfully and had me chortling with glee. Best Horror Story was easily my favorite. I really liked a number of the others, as well.<br /><br />What really got me, though, were the stories that, while emotionally engaging and had me chomping at the bit for more, just STOPPED. Anyone who has read these particular stories will know what I mean. Some of the best ended right where we should have gotten a full blow-by-blow and it was FINE in some cases. In others, it was like... wtf? What kind of end is that? Is he going to get blamed for a murder he didn't commit or not? Huh? lol<br /><br />All in all, the writing is solid as hell and fun and while the endings come and go, the beginnings and middles are all pretty much tops. :) I had fun, regardless.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2594305679" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13749308588301090624">This is my first ever Joe Hill book. Well, it is a collection of short stories to be exact.<br/><br/>For the single titles of the stories, please refer to my status updates, where you can also see the individual ratings. <br/>As you can see, not every story was a winner for me but the majority was great. And among those with a low(er) rating, I noticed that the writing itself was always of high quality. One simply didn't belong in a horror collection in my opinion and one or two other(s) seemed too short, like</span>
  <span id="freeText13749308588301090624" style="display:none">This is my first ever Joe Hill book. Well, it is a collection of short stories to be exact.<br /><br />For the single titles of the stories, please refer to my status updates, where you can also see the individual ratings. <br />As you can see, not every story was a winner for me but the majority was great. And among those with a low(er) rating, I noticed that the writing itself was always of high quality. One simply didn't belong in a horror collection in my opinion and one or two other(s) seemed too short, like a joke ending without the punch line.<br /><br />What was noticable in a number of stories was the subtlety with which the author delivered the supernatural kick to the reader's face. Like in the bonus story where I'm sure many a reader will have to go back to read one word at least twice before realizing what has been said. That is awesome! :D<br />And some stories showed clearly that this is Stephen King's son - the characterization of a few people here was fantastic in an infuriating sort of way that I only know from the King of Horror. *lol*<br /><br />Great atmosphere, distinct characters in often very creepy situations ... (almost) perfect Spooktober read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13749308588301090624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1495177528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1495177528" />
    <a title="Brandon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3214163-brandon"><img alt="Brandon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603283104p2/3214163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1495177528?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon" href="/user/show/3214163-brandon">Brandon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=short-story-collections">short-story-collections</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3214163-brandon?shelf=signed-by-author">signed-by-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1495177528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1711140341639613046">For a long time, this book was my “white whale”. Up to recently, I had three of Joe Hill’s novels (Heart-Shaped Box, Horns, NOS4A2) in hardcover and although 20th Century Ghosts was easily accessible in paperback or for download on my Kindle, I felt I needed to match my existing format and seek out the rare hardbound edition.<br/><br/>With every second-hand bookstore I entered, this illusive short story collection was front and centre in my mind. Unfortunately, I just couldn’t find it. A few months ago I </span>
  <span id="freeText1711140341639613046" style="display:none">For a long time, this book was my “white whale”. Up to recently, I had three of Joe Hill’s novels (Heart-Shaped Box, Horns, NOS4A2) in hardcover and although 20th Century Ghosts was easily accessible in paperback or for download on my Kindle, I felt I needed to match my existing format and seek out the rare hardbound edition.<br /><br />With every second-hand bookstore I entered, this illusive short story collection was front and centre in my mind. Unfortunately, I just couldn’t find it. A few months ago I had all but given up until my lovely girlfriend surprised me with a copy for Christmas! Did I mention it was signed? My name might not be “Tom” but who cares? Close enough.<br /><br />20th Century Ghosts is a collection of short stories dealing with the subjects of death, loss and fear. Although Joe Hill is an accomplished horror novelist, not everything in here will give you the willies. In fact, although one of the better stories - the collection’s namesake, 20th Century Ghost - features a spooky specter, it’s more nostalgic and sweet rather than scary.<br /><br />There are some great ones in here that I really enjoyed. “Best New Horror” tells of a magazine editor who has fallen into a rut - both professionally and personally - before coming across a new story so enthralling that it sets him on a quest to find its reclusive author. “Pop Art” did a real number on me and is probably my favourite of the bunch. It is a sad, coming of age tale about two friends who experience profound loss - did I mention one of them is an inflatable human?<br /><br />I still struggle with short story collections in that I’m so accustomed to long-form fiction that reading several tales in one session tends to burn me out a lot faster. I need to process a story after I finish one and then refocus on another. Maybe they’re better read in short bursts. I should probably adapt this strategy in the future.<br /><br />20th Century Ghosts is a glimpse into Hill’s past as several of the stories were written just as he was starting out as a published author - a time when he was relatively unknown and people didn’t know he was Uncle Stevie’s boy. It’s worth checking out for die hard fans and genre fiction enthusiasts alike.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1711140341639613046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222962230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10446893379475758081"><b>Loved it!!! Loved it!!!! Loved it!!!!!</b><br/><br/>These stories have got to be the best in any collection I have ever read. Hill's imagination is a wild ride I would stand in line for all day. I can't get enough from this man! Spooky, scary, frightening and just down right crazy... I love it all.<br/></span>

<div id="review_329329655" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329329655" />
    <a title="Edward Lorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn"><img alt="Edward Lorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492199506p2/8850418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/329329655?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Lorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Lorn" href="/user/show/8850418-edward-lorn">Edward Lorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780061447754" href="/book/show/1122026.20th_Century_Ghosts">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of literary horror</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer329329655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6536952024833656440">I first heard of Joe Hill after winning a bundle of used hardcovers on Ebay. Among the books in the lot were several King, some limited edition Laymon, a Straub or two, and two first editions by an author of which I had never heard. Heart Shaped Box and 20th Century Ghosts were in fantastic condition, and seemed to have never been read. I threw these two books on my shelf and forgot all about them. Later that year, I found a book at my local BAM entitled Horns. The premise piqued my interest, so</span>
  <span id="freeText6536952024833656440" style="display:none">I first heard of Joe Hill after winning a bundle of used hardcovers on Ebay. Among the books in the lot were several King, some limited edition Laymon, a Straub or two, and two first editions by an author of which I had never heard. Heart Shaped Box and 20th Century Ghosts were in fantastic condition, and seemed to have never been read. I threw these two books on my shelf and forgot all about them. Later that year, I found a book at my local BAM entitled Horns. The premise piqued my interest, so I bought the hardcover, devoured it over the course of three days, and then went to place the book on my shelf in alphabetic order by author. Low and behold, I laid eyes on two other books by the same writer, the aforementioned ones I'd won in the bundle, which I had forgotten all about. It wasn't until I finished Heart Shaped Box (which was in slightly less perfect condition in comparison to 20th Century Ghosts) and went in search of more Joe Hill goodness on Amazon that I found out that Hill is Stephen King's son. Surprise, surprise! The following year, Hill released NOS4A2 and cemented his place as one of my new favorite authors. Still, I hadn't read 20th Century Ghosts. You may be asking yourself why, and the answer is one that all book collectors can sympathize with: my hardcover copy is a first edition AND it is in pristine condition. I wouldn't crack that book open if you put a gun to my head. But I had to read it, of course I did, so I bought the ebook and added the audio book as a companion purchase. And let me tell you, the audio book is the way to go.<br /> <br />Notes on the Audible Edition: David Ledoux has a unique narrative voice. Every story herein is worth a read, but David's narration gives them that little something extra that just makes 'em pop. On the down side, the music, while thoroughly creepy, was produced at an entirely different volume than the spoken word. I had to turn down my device every time a story ended for fear of rupturing my ear drums when the musical intro for the next story began. Other than that, I recommend the Audible version.<br /> <br />Now, I will not be reviewing each and every story because they're all above average. I will warn that some of these tales have no ending. They are not open endings, or bad ending, but the stories of which I speak are completely devoid of any closing scene. They simply STOP. A perfect example of this is the opener, "Best New Horror", which is also one of the best stories in the collection. My absolute favorite story in the collection is "Pop Art", and that's because of the sheer originality of the tale. Going into 20th Century Ghosts, I knew nothing about "Pop Art", and I hope you have the same option. Even giving you a brief, vague synopsis would ruin the experience, so I will move on. Other notable tales are "20th Century Ghost", "The Black Phone", "The Cape" (which has one of the best endings I've ever read where short stories are concerned), and the uber imaginative "Voluntary Committal". The only story I disliked was "Abraham's Boys". Hill tried too damn hard to breathe new life into overused, out-of-date characters, and it resulted in the book's only failure. That one story slowed the book down and gummed up the collection's works so badly I was compelled to knock off a full star from the overall rating. Yes, it was that bad. If I had to rate "Abraham's Boys" on its own, I would give it two stars. Everything else gets fours and fives. <br /> <br />You should know that these are not short stories; not technically anyway. Hill suffers the same what-ails-him as his father. Neither man seems to grasp the concept of "short", but they write so damn well that you barely notice that their outings are more along the line of novelettes and novellas than they are short stories. <br /> <br />In summation: A terrific collection of longer short stories, and far more literary in scope than your average horror anthology. If you're a fan of Stephen King's Skeleton Crew or Neil Gaiman's Smoke and Mirrors, you should enjoy 20th Century Ghosts. <br /> <br />(A Note to Completionists: Hill hides a short story in the afterward, so make sure you don't skip the acknowledgment section. You're welcome.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="6536952024833656440" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58009676" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58009676" />
    <a title="Chris " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1956959-chris"><img alt="Chris " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502408626p2/1956959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58009676?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris " href="/user/show/1956959-chris">Chris </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58009676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15328417292011200396">Wow. What better word to start this review? Joe Hill has a true gift for the short story.<br/><br/>All short story collections are somewhat hit and miss with me. Some stories are great, some good, some okay, and some meh. That's pretty much the standard with even my favorite short story authors: Stephen King, Neil Gaiman, George R.R. Martin. But I'm definitely putting Joe Hill on that list now. His stories vary as well between excellent and meh, but even the "meh" category stories are written with his sub</span>
  <span id="freeText15328417292011200396" style="display:none">Wow. What better word to start this review? Joe Hill has a true gift for the short story.<br /><br />All short story collections are somewhat hit and miss with me. Some stories are great, some good, some okay, and some meh. That's pretty much the standard with even my favorite short story authors: Stephen King, Neil Gaiman, George R.R. Martin. But I'm definitely putting Joe Hill on that list now. His stories vary as well between excellent and meh, but even the "meh" category stories are written with his subtle flare. It cannot be denied that his stories flow smoothly, and this goes for the best as well as the worst of the lot.<br /><br />There really aren't any bad stories in this group. Some are better than others, and a few left me unsatisfied. But even those were engaging. Even the two page "Dead-Wood" was beautifully crafted and left me feeling like I had gained something. And the best of the lot? Oh, man. Those were solidly awesome. "Pop Art", "Abraham's Boys", "Bobby Conroy Comes Back from the Dead", "My Father's Mask", "Voluntary Committal", and "Better Than Home" were stories that will stick with me for a long time indeed.<br /><br />Often when I read a collection, certain stories make me wonder if I've wasted my time. 20th Century Ghosts did not. In fact, it made me want more. Luckily, Joe's new novel is due out tomorrow.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15328417292011200396" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_4670939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4670939" />
    <a title="Peggy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/275001-peggy"><img alt="Peggy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187021327p2/275001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4670939?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peggy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peggy" href="/user/show/275001-peggy">Peggy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4670939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16901592265982428537">I love Fall. Football has returned to the airwaves. The leaves are changing colors, and there’s a cold snap in the…oh, who am I kidding. This is Texas. If we’re lucky, it will only be 85 degrees with 95% humidity as you’re reading this. Still, I really do like the <b>
  <i>idea</i>
</b> of Fall, though, and if I have to make my own chill, well, then, so be it. The best way I know of to bring a chill to the air is to break out the scary stories. Luckily for us, one of the finest collections of contemporary hor</span>
  <span id="freeText16901592265982428537" style="display:none">I love Fall. Football has returned to the airwaves. The leaves are changing colors, and there’s a cold snap in the…oh, who am I kidding. This is Texas. If we’re lucky, it will only be 85 degrees with 95% humidity as you’re reading this. Still, I really do like the <b>
  <i>idea</i>
</b> of Fall, though, and if I have to make my own chill, well, then, so be it. The best way I know of to bring a chill to the air is to break out the scary stories. Luckily for us, one of the finest collections of contemporary horror stories has just been released in the US.<br /><br />Some of you may have picked up a debut novel this past Spring called <i>Heart-Shaped Box</i> by Joe Hill (If you didn’t, go ahead and grab one now. I’ll wait.). It was a terrific book: fast-paced, subtle, surprisingly touching, and truly creepy. But you might not know that way back in 2005 a British publisher called PS Publishing took note of a spate of award-winning stories by a new American author and collected them all together into a book called <i>20th Century Ghosts</i>. The book garnered many award mentions and landed on numerous best-of lists, but it was only available in Britain. Darn greedy Brits. Lucky for us, an American version is finally available, just in time to add some spice to our Fall. As a bonus, because we had to wait so long, we get an extra story not included in the British edition. Take that, greedy Brits!<br /><br />Joe Hill is a mighty-fine writer, and this collection really showcases his talent. Are you looking for a sweet and subtle ghost story with some cogent points to make about aging and regret? Look no further than “20th Century Ghost.” Did you ever wonder what it would be like if you introduced Franz Kafka to the Nuclear Age (or just have a fondness for giant bugs)? Check out “You Will Hear the Locust Sing.” Maybe you favor a meditation on friendship and persecution? Take a look at “Pop Art,” which Christopher Golden (rightly) called transcendent. But wait a minute. I was talking about chills, wasn’t I? Maybe that’s what you want; the hard stuff. Like “Black Phone,” where a small child trapped in a very bad place receives phone calls from the dead. Or “Last Breath,” which starts out creepy and then sinks into downright disturbing. No, I know. You need a harsh story. A modern story. A story that actually uses your knowledge of horror conventions to build its atmosphere. You need “Year’s Best Horror.”<br /><br /><i>20th Century Ghosts</i> is a terrific collection, and one that really makes use of Joe Hill’s talents. The stories differ in tone but share a style and subtlety and emotional impact – Hill weaves a web of such dark beauty that you long to be ensnared.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16901592265982428537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2327495442" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2327495442" />
    <a title="Jamie Stewart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15954768-jamie-stewart"><img alt="Jamie Stewart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551013124p2/15954768.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2327495442?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie Stewart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie Stewart" href="/user/show/15954768-jamie-stewart">Jamie Stewart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2327495442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10026000033702194185">This was a reread for me and it will always be something special to me. It earned a place on my bookshelf as the best short story collection I had ever read. I often found such collections jarring in the past because of the ever changing tones and paces that can happen when reading collections. However, when I read this book for the first time I was engrossed and found every tale just as engaging as the next. That was then and this is now. <br/><br/>I dropped my rating by one star because while their are </span>
  <span id="freeText10026000033702194185" style="display:none">This was a reread for me and it will always be something special to me. It earned a place on my bookshelf as the best short story collection I had ever read. I often found such collections jarring in the past because of the ever changing tones and paces that can happen when reading collections. However, when I read this book for the first time I was engrossed and found every tale just as engaging as the next. That was then and this is now. <br /><br />I dropped my rating by one star because while their are masterfully told stories here not all of them are of the same quality. 20th Century Ghost and Pop Art are masterpieces, filled with horror and sorrow and regret and delight. The rest unfortunately don’t come close to the high standard set by them. However, if you haven’t read this collection I think it’s very worth while, especially as a horror fan. I also feel it’s a collection that can be offered to non horror fans and they’d enjoy it. Joe Hill has a fantastic talent for making you care, for writing his tales that despite how grotesque they are you root for someone in them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10026000033702194185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="joyce g" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53140602-joyce-g"><img alt="joyce g" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488666954p2/53140602.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_141377282" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/141377282" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4677640-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472613597p2/4677640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/141377282?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/4677640-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6570601-20th-century-ghosts">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of short stories, coming of age tales, and horror stories</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=cool-concept">cool-concept</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=short-stuff">short-stuff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4677640-richard?shelf=lovey-dovey">lovey-dovey</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer141377282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14941201398648600685"><b>*Re-Read (audio) in 2015 (originally read in 2010)*</b><br/><br/>4.5 stars<br/>This is a great story collection, which is interesting, because I was pretty disappointed by author Joe Hill's first two novels, <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/153025.Heart_Shaped_Box' title='Heart-Shaped Box by Joe Hill' rel='nofollow noopener'>Heart-Shaped Box</a>
</i> and <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6587879.Horns' title='Horns by Joe Hill' rel='nofollow noopener'>Horns</a>
</i>. I thought they both started very strong and then fizzled about half-way through. Maybe Hill is better writing in the short form?<br/><br/>The theme running most of these stories are kids coming of age through unexplained events and phenomenon that will stay with them forever, even as </span>
  <span id="freeText14941201398648600685" style="display:none"><b>*Re-Read (audio) in 2015 (originally read in 2010)*</b><br /><br />4.5 stars<br />This is a great story collection, which is interesting, because I was pretty disappointed by author Joe Hill's first two novels, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/153025.Heart_Shaped_Box" title="Heart-Shaped Box by Joe Hill" rel="nofollow noopener">Heart-Shaped Box</a>
</i> and <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6587879.Horns" title="Horns by Joe Hill" rel="nofollow noopener">Horns</a>
</i>. I thought they both started very strong and then fizzled about half-way through. Maybe Hill is better writing in the short form?<br /><br />The theme running most of these stories are kids coming of age through unexplained events and phenomenon that will stay with them forever, even as they get older. There's a romantic, nostalgic quality to the writing that really resonates throughout, and Hill is really gifted at capturing the voice of a young boy. The title story, "20th Century Ghost," is one of the best short stories I've read. It really touched me and sets the standard for the whole collection. Other standout tales from the collection are "Pop Art," "The Cape," "Last Breath," "Bobby Conroy Comes Back From The Dead," and the great novella that closes the collection, "Voluntary Committal." And "My Father's Mask" is so uncomfortably disturbing!<br /><br />I decided to try my hand at audiobooks for the first time to help me through long commutes, and I thought re-reading this collection would be a great way to start. The reader was engaging and was great at voicing the kids. I would recommend this to anyone that enjoys coming of age tales and horror stories. This is a wonderful collection and deserves to be right up there with the great collections from Richard Matheson and Stephen King.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14941201398648600685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
